<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Sidebar_periodic_table" title="Template:Sidebar periodic table"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Template_talk:Sidebar_periodic_table" title="Template talk:Sidebar periodic table"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Sidebar_periodic_table&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>An <b>extended periodic table</b> theorizes about elements beyond <a href="/wiki/Oganesson" title="Oganesson">oganesson</a> (beyond period 7, or row 7). Currently seven <a href="/wiki/Period_(periodic_table)" title="Period (periodic table)">periods</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_elements" title="Chemical elements">chemical elements</a> are known and proven, culminating with <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> <a href="/wiki/Oganesson" title="Oganesson">118</a>, which completes the seventh row.</p>
<p>If further elements with higher atomic numbers than this are discovered, they will be placed in additional periods, laid out (as with the existing periods) to illustrate periodically recurring trends in the properties of the elements concerned. Any additional periods are expected to contain a larger number of elements than the seventh period, as they are calculated to have an additional so-called <b>g-block</b>, containing at least 18 elements with partially filled g-<a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">orbitals</a> in each period.</p>
<p>An <b>eight-period table</b> containing this block was suggested by <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a> in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUPAC" title="IUPAC">IUPAC</a> defines an element to exist if its lifetime is longer than 10<sup>−14</sup> seconds, which is the time it takes for the nucleus to form an electron cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> No elements in this region have been synthesized or discovered in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The first element of the g-block may have <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 121, and thus would have the <a href="/wiki/Systematic_element_name" title="Systematic element name">systematic name</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unbiunium" title="Unbiunium">unbiunium</a>. Elements in this region are likely to be highly unstable with respect to <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>, and have extremely short <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half_life" title="Half life">half lives</a>, although element 126 is hypothesized to be within an <a href="/wiki/Island_of_stability" title="Island of stability">island of stability</a> that is resistant to fission but not to <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a>. It is not clear how many elements beyond the expected island of stability are physically possible, whether period 8 is complete, or if there is a period 9.</p>
<p>According to the orbital approximation in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical</a> descriptions of atomic structure, the g-block would correspond to elements with partially filled g-orbitals, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-orbit_coupling" title="Spin-orbit coupling">spin-orbit coupling</a> effects reduce the validity of the orbital approximation substantially for elements of high <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>. While Seaborg's version of the extended period had the heavier elements following the pattern set by lighter elements, as it did not take into account <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_quantum_chemistry" title="Relativistic quantum chemistry">relativistic effects</a>, models that take relativistic effects into account do not. <a href="/wiki/Pekka_Pyykk%C3%B6" title="Pekka Pyykkö">Pekka Pyykkö</a> and B. Fricke used computer modeling to calculate the positions of elements up to <i><a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">Z</a></i> = 184, and found that several were displaced from the <a href="/wiki/Aufbau_principle#The_Madelung_energy_ordering_rule" title="Aufbau principle">Madelung rule</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rsc.org_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-rsc.org-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Feynman" title="Richard Feynman">Richard Feynman</a> noted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elert_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elert-7">[7]</a></sup> that a simplistic interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">relativistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Dirac_equation" title="Dirac equation">Dirac equation</a> runs into problems with electron orbitals at <i>Z</i> &gt; 1/α ≈ 137 as described in the sections below, suggesting that neutral atoms cannot exist beyond <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Untriseptium" title="Untriseptium">untriseptium</a>, and that a periodic table of elements based on electron orbitals therefore breaks down at this point. On the other hand, a more rigorous analysis calculates the limit to be <i>Z</i> ≈ 173.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It is unknown how far the periodic table might extend beyond the known 118 elements. <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a> suggested that the highest possible element may be under <i>Z</i> = 130,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB-8">[8]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Walter_Greiner" title="Walter Greiner">Walter Greiner</a> predicted that there may not be a highest possible element. The table below shows one possibility for the appearance of the eighth period, with placement of elements primarily based on their predicted chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rsc_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-rsc-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb" style="table-layout:fixed; margin:5px 0; clear:both; width:100%;">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="table-layout:fixed; overflow:auto;">
<table border="0" cellpadding="0" class="" style="table-format:fixed; width:100%;max-width:1300px; font-size:85%; border:1px solid #ccc;">

<p>All of these hypothetical undiscovered elements are named by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC) <a href="/wiki/Systematic_element_name" title="Systematic element name">systematic element name</a> standard which creates a generic name for use until the element has been discovered, confirmed, and an official name approved. These names are typically not used in the literature, and are referred to by their atomic numbers; hence, element 164 would usually not be called "unhexquadium" (the IUPAC systematic name), but rather "element 164" with symbol "164", "(164)", or "E164".</p>
<p>As of April 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, synthesis has been attempted for only <a href="/wiki/Ununennium" title="Ununennium">ununennium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unbinilium" title="Unbinilium">unbinilium</a>, unbibium, unbiquadium, unbihexium, and unbiseptium. (Z = 119, 120, 122, 124, 126, and 127)</p>
<p>At element 118, the orbitals 1s, 2s, 2p, 3s, 3p, 3d, 4s, 4p, 4d, 4f, 5s, 5p, 5d, 5f, 6s, 6p, 6d, 7s and 7p are assumed to be filled, with the remaining orbitals unfilled. A simple extrapolation from the <a href="/wiki/Aufbau_principle" title="Aufbau principle">Aufbau principle</a> would predict the eighth row to fill orbitals in the order 8s, 5g, 6f, 7d, 8p; but after element 120, the proximity of the electron shells makes placement in a simple table problematic. Although a simple extrapolation of the periodic table, following Seaborg's original concept, would put the elements after 120 as follows: 121-138 form the g-block superactinoids; 139-152 form the f-block superactinoids, 153-162 would be transition metals; 163-166 post-transition metals; 167=halogen; 168=noble gas; 169=alkali metal; 170=alkaline earth metal, Dirac-Fock calculations predict that it will most likely go: 121-142 form the g-block superactinoids; 143-156 form the f-block superactinoids; 157-166 form the transition metals; 167-170 post-transition metals; 171=halogen; 172=noble gas.</p>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>needs attention from an expert on the subject</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please add a <i>reason</i> or a <i>talk</i> parameter to this template to explain the issue with the article. Consider <a href="/wiki/Template:Expert_needed" title="Template:Expert needed">associating this request</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject">WikiProject</a>.</span> <small><i>(December 2011)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><span id="Pyykk.C3.B6_model"></span> Not all models show the higher elements following the pattern established by lighter elements. <a href="/wiki/Pekka_Pyykk%C3%B6" title="Pekka Pyykkö">Pekka Pyykkö</a>, for example, used computer modeling to calculate the positions of elements up to Z=172, and found that several were displaced from the <a href="/wiki/Aufbau_principle" title="Aufbau principle">Madelung energy-ordering rule</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rsc.org_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-rsc.org-6">[6]</a></sup> He predicts that the orbital shells will fill up in this order:</p>
<ul>
<li>8s,</li>
<li>5g,</li>
<li>the first two spaces of 8p,</li>
<li>6f,</li>
<li>7d,</li>
<li>9s,</li>
<li>the first two spaces of 9p,</li>
<li>the rest of 8p.</li>
</ul>
<p>He also suggests that period 8 be split into three parts:</p>
<ul>
<li>8a, containing 8s,</li>
<li>8b, containing the first two elements of 8p,</li>
<li>8c, containing 7d and the rest of 8p.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT172_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-PT172-10">[10]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Fricke <i>et al.</i> also predicted the extended periodic table up to 184.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup> This model has been more widely used among scientists and is shown above as the main form of the extended periodic table.</p>

</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predicted_properties_of_eighth-period_elements">Predicted properties of eighth-period elements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Predicted properties of eighth-period elements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Element 118 is the last element that has been synthesized. The next two elements, <a href="/wiki/Ununennium" title="Ununennium">elements 119</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unbinilium" title="Unbinilium">120</a>, should form an 8s series and be an <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metal</a> respectively. Beyond element 120, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superactinide" title="Superactinide">superactinide</a> series is expected to begin, when the 8s electrons and the filling 8p<sub>1/2</sub>, 7d<sub>3/2</sub>, 6f<sub>5/2</sub>, and 5g<sub>7/2</sub> subshells determine the chemistry of these elements. Complete and accurate CCSD calculations are not available for elements beyond 122 because of the extreme complexity of the situation: the 5g, 6f, and 7d orbitals should have about the same energy level, and in the region of element 160 the 9s, 8p<sub>3/2</sub>, and 9p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals should also be about equal in energy. This will cause the electron shells to mix so that the <a href="/wiki/Block_(periodic_table)" title="Block (periodic table)">block</a> concept no longer applies very well, and will also result in novel chemical properties that will make positioning these elements in a periodic table very difficult. For example, element 164 is expected to mix characteristics of the elements of <a href="/wiki/Group_10_element" title="Group 10 element">group 10</a>, <a href="/wiki/Group_12_element" title="Group 12 element">12</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_group" title="Carbon group">14</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">18</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_and_physical_properties">Chemical and physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chemical and physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="8s_elements">8s elements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 8s elements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ununennium" title="Ununennium">Ununennium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unbinilium" title="Unbinilium">Unbinilium</a></div>
<div style="float: right; margin: 1px; font-size:85%;">

<p>The first two elements of period 8 are expected to be <a href="/wiki/Ununennium" title="Ununennium">ununennium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unbinilium" title="Unbinilium">unbinilium</a>, elements 119 and 120. Their <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configurations</a> should have the 8s orbital being filled. This orbital is relativistically stabilized and contracted and thus, elements 119 and 120 should be more like <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">rubidium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a> than their immediate neighbours above, <a href="/wiki/Francium" title="Francium">francium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>. Another effect of the relativistic contraction of the 8s orbital is that the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_radius" title="Atomic radius">atomic radii</a> of these two elements should be about the same of those of francium and radium. They should behave like normal <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metals</a>, normally forming +1 and +2 <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> respectively, but the relativistic destabilization of the 7p<sub>3/2</sub> subshell and the relatively low <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization energies</a> of the 7p<sub>3/2</sub> electrons should make higher oxidation states like +3 and +4 (respectively) possible as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Superactinides">Superactinides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Superactinides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The superactinide series is expected to contain elements 121 to 157. In the superactinide series, the 7d<sub>3/2</sub>, 8p<sub>1/2</sub>, 6f<sub>5/2</sub> and 5g<sub>7/2</sub> shells should all fill simultaneously:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-12">[12]</a></sup> this creates very complicated situations, so much so that complete and accurate CCSD calculations have been done only for elements 121 and 122.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup> The first superactinide, unbiunium (element 121), should be a <a href="/wiki/Congener_(chemistry)" title="Congener (chemistry)">congener</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum" title="Lanthanum">lanthanum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actinium" title="Actinium">actinium</a> and should have similar properties to them:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> its main oxidation state should be +3, although the closeness of the valence subshells' energy levels may permit higher oxidation states, just like in elements 119 and 120.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup> Relativistic stabilization of the 8p subshell should result in a ground-state 8s<sup>2</sup>8p<sup>1</sup> valence electron configuration for element 121, in contrast to the ds<sup>2</sup> configurations of lanthanum and actinium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup> Its first ionization energy is predicted to be 429.4 kJ/mol, which would be lower than those of all known elements except for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_metals" title="Alkali metals">alkali metals</a> <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">rubidium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Francium" title="Francium">francium</a>: this value is even lower than that of the period 8 alkali metal ununennium (463.1 kJ/mol). Similarly, the next superactinide, unbibium (element 122), may be a congener of <a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">cerium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, with a main oxidation state of +4, but would have a ground-state 7d<sup>1</sup>8s<sup>2</sup>8p<sup>1</sup> valence electron configuration, unlike thorium's 6d<sup>2</sup>7s<sup>2</sup> configuration. Hence, its first <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization energy</a> would be smaller than thorium's (Th: 6.54 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a>; Ubb: 5.6 eV) because of the greater ease of ionizing unbibium's 8p<sub>1/2</sub> electron than thorium's 7s electron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the first few superactinides, the binding energies of the added electrons are predicted to be small enough that they can lose all their valence electrons; for example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unbihexium" title="Unbihexium">unbihexium</a> (element 126) could easily form a +8 oxidation state, and even higher oxidation states for the next few elements may be possible. Unbihexium is also predicted to display a variety of other <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a>: recent calculations have suggested a stable <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">monofluoride</a> UbhF may be possible, resulting from a bonding interaction between the 5g <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">orbital</a> on unbihexium and the 2<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-orbital" title="P-orbital">p</a> orbital on <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacoby_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacoby-14">[14]</a></sup> Other predicted oxidation states include +2, +4, and +6; +4 is expected to be the most usual oxidation state of unbihexium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-12">[12]</a></sup> The presence of electrons in g-orbitals, which do not exist in the ground state electron configuration of any currently known element, should allow presently unknown <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" title="Orbital hybridization">hybrid</a> orbitals to form and influence the chemistry of the superactinides in new ways, although the absence of <i>g</i> electrons in known elements makes predicting their chemistry more difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup></p>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>In the later superactinides, the oxidation states should become lower. By element 132, the predominant most stable oxidation state will be only +6; this is further reduced to +3 and +4 by element 144, and at the end of the superactinide series it will be only +2 (and possibly even 0) because the 6f shell, which is being filled at that point, is deep inside the electron cloud and the 8s and 8p<sub>1/2</sub> electrons are bound too strongly to be chemically active. The 5g shell should be filled at element 144 and the 6f shell at around element 154, and at this region of the superactinides the 8p<sub>1/2</sub> electrons are bound so strongly that they are no longer active chemically, so that only a few electrons can participate in chemical reactions. Calculations by Fricke <i>et al.</i> predict that at element 154, the 6f shell is full and there are no d- or other electron <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave functions</a> outside the chemically inactive 8s and 8p<sub>1/2</sub> shells. This would cause element 154 to be very <a href="/wiki/Reactivity_(chemistry)" title="Reactivity (chemistry)">unreactive</a>, so that it may exhibit properties similar to those of the noble gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Similarly to the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide_contraction" title="Lanthanide contraction">lanthanide and actinide contractions</a>, there should be a superactinide contraction in the superactinide series where the <a href="/wiki/Ionic_radius" title="Ionic radius">ionic radii</a> of the superactinides are smaller than expected. In the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a>, the contraction is about 4.4 pm per element; in the <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a>, it is about 3 pm per element. The contraction is larger in the lanthanides than in the actinides due to the greater localization of the 4f wave function as compared to the 5f wave function. Comparisons with the wave functions of the outer electrons of the lanthanides, actinides, and superactinides lead to a prediction of a contraction of about 2 pm per element in the superactinides; although this is smaller than the contractions in the lanthanides and actinides, its total effect is larger due to the fact that 32 electrons are filled in the deeply buried 5g and 6f shells, instead of just 14 electrons being filled in the 4f and 5f shells in the lanthanides and actinides respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pekka_Pyykk%C3%B6" title="Pekka Pyykkö">Pekka Pyykkö</a> divides these superactinides into three series: a 5g series (elements 121 to 138), an 8p<sub>1/2</sub> series (elements 139 to 140), and a 6f series (elements 141 to 155), although noting that there would be a great deal of overlapping between energy levels and that the 6f, 7d, or 8p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals could well also be occupied in the early superactinide atoms or ions. He also expects that they would behave more like "super<a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a>", in the sense that the 5g electrons would mostly be chemically inactive, similarly to how only one or two 4f electrons in each lanthanide are ever ionized in chemical compounds. He also predicted that the possible oxidation states of the superactinides might rise very high in the 6f series, to values such as +12 in element 148.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT172_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-PT172-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>As an example from the late superactinides, element 156 is expected to exhibit mainly the +2 oxidation state. Its first ionization energy should be about 395.6 kJ/mol and its metallic radius should be about 170 picometers. It should be a very heavy metal with a density of around 26 g/cm<sup>3</sup>. Its relative atomic mass should be around 445 u.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="7d_transition_metals">7d transition metals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 7d transition metals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The transition metals in period 8 are expected to be elements 157 to 166. Although the 8s and 8p<sub>1/2</sub> electrons are bound so strongly in these elements that they should not be able to take part in any chemical reactions, the 9s and 9p<sub>1/2</sub> levels are expected to be readily available for hybridization such that these elements will still behave chemically like their lighter <a href="/wiki/Homology_(chemistry)" title="Homology (chemistry)">homologues</a> in the periodic table, showing the same oxidation states as they do, in contrast to earlier predictions which predicted the period 8 transition metals to have main oxidation states two less than those of their lighter congeners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The noble metals of this series of transition metals are not expected to be as noble as their lighter homologues, due to the absence of an outer <i>s</i> shell for shielding and also because the 7d shell is strongly split into two subshells due to relativistic effects. This causes the first ionization energies of the 7d transition metals to be smaller than those of their lighter congeners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-9"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Calculations predict that the 7d electrons of element 164 (unhexquadium) should participate very readily in chemical reactions, so that unhexquadium should be able to show stable +6 and +4 oxidation states in addition to the normal +2 state in <a href="/wiki/Aqueous_solution" title="Aqueous solution">aqueous solutions</a> with strong <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a>. Unhexquadium should thus be able to form compounds like Uhq(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonyl" title="Carbonyl">CO</a>)<sub>4</sub>, Uhq(<a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_trifluoride" title="Phosphorus trifluoride">PF<sub>3</sub></a>)<sub>4</sub> (both <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Tetrahedral molecular geometry">tetrahedral</a>), and <span class="chemf nowrap">Uhq(<a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">CN</a>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2−<br/>
2</span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Linear_molecular_geometry" title="Linear molecular geometry">linear</a>), which is very different behavior from that of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, which unhexquadium would be a heavier <a href="/wiki/Homology_(chemistry)" title="Homology (chemistry)">homologue</a> of if not for relativistic effects. Nevertheless, the divalent state would be the main one in aqueous solution, and unhexquadium(II) should behave more similarly to lead than unhexquadium(IV) and unhexquadium(VI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-10"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unhexquadium should be a <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">soft</a> metal like mercury, and metallic unhexquadium should have a high melting point as it is predicted to bond <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalently</a>. It is also expected to be a soft <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a> and have <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ahrlands_softness_parameter&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ahrlands softness parameter (page does not exist)">Ahrlands softness parameter</a> close to 4 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a>. It should also have some similarities to <a href="/wiki/Oganesson" title="Oganesson">oganesson</a> as well as to the other group 12 elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-11"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup> Unhexquadium should be at most moderately reactive, having a first ionization energy that should be around 685 kJ/mol, comparable to that of <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-12">[12]</a></sup> Due to the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide_contraction" title="Lanthanide contraction">lanthanide, actinide, and superactinide contractions</a>, unhexquadium should have a metallic radius of only 158 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picometer" title="Picometer">pm</a>, very close to that of the much lighter <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, despite its being expected to have an atomic weight of around 474 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">u</a>, about 19.5 times as much as that of magnesium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-11"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup> This small radius and high weight cause it to be expected to have an extremely high density of around 46 g·cm<sup>−3</sup>, over twice that of <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a>, currently the most dense element known, at 22.61 g·cm<sup>−3</sup>; unhexquadium should be the second most dense element in the first 172 elements in the periodic table, with only its neighbour unhextrium (element 163) being more dense (at 47 g·cm<sup>−3</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-12"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup> Metallic unhexquadium should be quite stable, as the 8s and 8p<sub>1/2</sub> electrons are very deeply buried in the electron core and only the 7d electrons are available for bonding. Metallic unhexquadium should have a very large <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cohesive_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cohesive energy (page does not exist)">cohesive energy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Enthalpy" title="Enthalpy">enthalpy</a> of crystallization) due to its covalent bonds, most probably resulting in a high melting point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Theoretical interest in the chemistry of unhexquadium is largely motivated by theoretical predictions that it, especially the isotope <sup>482</sup>Uhq (with 164 <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and 318 <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>), would be at the center of a hypothetical second <a href="/wiki/Island_of_stability" title="Island of stability">island of stability</a> (the first being centered on <sup>306</sup><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unbibium" title="Unbibium">Ubb</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kratz_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kratz-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurekalert.org_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-eurekalert.org-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Elements 165 (unhexpentium) and 166 (unhexhexium), the last two 7d transition metals, should behave similarly to <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metals</a> when in the +1 and +2 oxidation states respectively. The 9s electrons should have ionization energies comparable to those of the 3s electrons of <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, due to relativistic effects causing the 9s electrons to be much more strongly bound than non-relativistic calculations would predict. Elements 165 and 166 should normally exhibit the +1 and +2 oxidation states respectively, although the ionization energies of the 7d electrons are low enough to allow higher oxidation states like +3 for element 165, though the oxidation state +4 for element 166 is less likely (similar to the lighter group 12 elements).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-13"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haire_11-12"><a href="#cite_note-Haire-11">[11]</a></sup></p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Elements_167_to_172">Elements 167 to 172 <span id="Elements_165_to_172"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Elements 167 to 172">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The next six elements on the periodic table should be the last six main-group elements before the end of the periodic table at <i>Z</i> = 173.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT172_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-PT172-10">[10]</a></sup> In elements 167 to 172, the 9p<sub>1/2</sub> and 8p<sub>3/2</sub> shells will be filled. Their energy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eigenvalue" title="Eigenvalue">eigenvalues</a> are so close together that they behave as one combined <i>p</i> shell, similar to the non-relativistic 2p and 3p shells. Thus, the <a href="/wiki/Inert_pair_effect" title="Inert pair effect">inert pair effect</a> does not occur and the most common oxidation states of elements 167 to 170 should be +3, +4, +5, and +6 respectively. Element 171 (unseptunium) is expected to show some similarities to the <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a>, showing various oxidation states ranging from –1 to +7, although its physical properties should be closer to that of a metal; it has hence been coloured as a metalloid. Its electron affinity should be 3.0 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a>, allowing it to form HUsu, analogous to a hydrogen halide. The Usu<sup>−</sup> ion is expected to be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HSAB" title="HSAB">soft base</a>, comparable to <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">iodide</a> (I<sup>−</sup>). Element 172 (unseptbium) should be a <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> with chemical behaviour similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a>, as their ionization energies should be very similar (Xe, 1170.4 kJ/mol; Usb, 1090.3 kJ/mol). The only main difference between them is that element 172, unlike xenon, is expected to be a <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a> due to its much higher atomic weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-15"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup> Unseptbium should be a strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a>, forming fluorides and oxides, similarly to its lighter congener xenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke_12-7"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-12">[12]</a></sup> Because of this analogy of elements 165–172 to periods 2 and 3, Fricke <i>et al.</i> considered them to form a ninth period of the periodic table, while the eighth period was taken by them to end at the noble metal element 164. This ninth and final period would be similar to the second and third period in that it should have no transition metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke_12-8"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<div style="float: center; margin: 1px; font-size:85%;">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Beyond_element_172">Beyond element 172 <span id=".22Eka-superactinides.22"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Beyond element 172">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Immediately after element 172 (unseptbium, the last period 8 element), the first noble gas after <a href="/wiki/Oganesson" title="Oganesson">oganesson</a> (the last period 7 element), it was originally expected that another long transition series like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superactinide" title="Superactinide">superactinides</a> should begin, filling the 6g, 7f, 8d, and perhaps 6h shells. These electrons would be very loosely bound, rendering extremely high oxidation states possibly easy to reach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke_12-10"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-12">[12]</a></sup> Element 184 (unoctquadium) was significantly targeted in early predictions, as it was originally speculated that 184 would be a proton magic number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke_5-17"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke_12-11"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penneman_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Penneman-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, these extrapolations are unlikely to be fulfilled, due to the impending end of the periodic table at <i>Z</i> = 173.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT172_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-PT172-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In element 173 (unsepttrium), the last electron would enter the 6g<sub>7/2</sub> subshell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke1977_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke1977-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_the_periodic_table">End of the periodic table</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: End of the periodic table">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The number of physically possible elements is unknown. A low estimate is that the periodic table may end soon after the <a href="/wiki/Island_of_stability" title="Island of stability">island of stability</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB-8">[8]</a></sup> which is expected to center on <i>Z</i> = 126, as the extension of the periodic and nuclides tables is restricted by the proton and the neutron <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_drip_line" title="Nuclear drip line">drip lines</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> some, such as Walter Greiner, predict that there may not be an end to the periodic table.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rsc_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-rsc-9">[9]</a></sup> Other predictions of an end to the periodic table include <i>Z</i> = 128 (<a href="/wiki/John_Emsley" title="John Emsley">John Emsley</a>) and <i>Z</i> = 155 (Albert Khazan).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Feynmanium_and_elements_above_the_atomic_number_137">Feynmanium and elements above the atomic number 137</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Feynmanium and elements above the atomic number 137">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Feynman" title="Richard Feynman">Richard Feynman</a> noted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elert_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elert-7">[7]</a></sup> that a simplistic interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">relativistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Dirac_equation" title="Dirac equation">Dirac equation</a> runs into problems with electron orbitals at <i>Z</i> &gt; 1/α ≈ 137 as described in the sections below, suggesting that neutral atoms cannot exist beyond <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Untriseptium" title="Untriseptium">untriseptium</a>, and that a periodic table of elements based on electron orbitals therefore breaks down at this point. On the other hand, a more rigorous analysis calculates the limit to be <i>Z</i> ≈ 173.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Bohr_model">Bohr model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bohr model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">Bohr model</a> exhibits difficulty for atoms with atomic number greater than 137, for the speed of an electron in a <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">1s electron orbital</a>, <i>v</i>, is given by</p>
<dl>

</math></span><img alt="v=Z\alpha c\approx {\frac {Zc}{137.036}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d43fa4c6b48eb5be3864581ab34cc5d383985f27" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:20.093ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>Z</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>, and <i>α</i> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fine_structure_constant" title="Fine structure constant">fine structure constant</a>, a measure of the strength of electromagnetic interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Under this approximation, any element with an atomic number of greater than 137 would require 1s electrons to be traveling faster than <i>c</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. Hence the non-relativistic Bohr model is clearly inaccurate when applied to such an element.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Relativistic_Dirac_equation">Relativistic Dirac equation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Relativistic Dirac equation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">relativistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Dirac_equation" title="Dirac equation">Dirac equation</a> gives the ground state energy as</p>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E={\frac {mc^{2}}{\sqrt {1+{\frac {Z^{2}\alpha ^{2}}{n-(j+1/2)+{\sqrt {(j+1/2)^{2}-Z^{2}\alpha ^{2}}}}}}}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle E={\frac {mc^{2}}{\sqrt {1+{\frac {Z^{2}\alpha ^{2}}{n-(j+1/2)+{\sqrt {(j+1/2)^{2}-Z^{2}\alpha ^{2}}}}}}}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/115c653ddd3d1fc247ab43d3c6dccbef2ee97f2d" style="vertical-align: -7.505ex; width:36.378ex; height:11.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>m</i> is the rest mass of the electron. For <i>Z</i> &gt; 137, the wave function of the Dirac ground state is oscillatory, rather than bound, and there is no gap between the positive and negative energy spectra, as in the <a href="/wiki/Klein_paradox" title="Klein paradox">Klein paradox</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> More accurate calculations taking into account the effects of the finite size of the nucleus indicate that the binding energy first exceeds 2<i>mc</i><sup>2</sup> for <i>Z</i> &gt; <i>Z</i><sub>cr</sub> ≈ 173. For <i>Z</i> &gt; <i>Z</i><sub>cr</sub>, if the innermost orbital (1s) is not filled, the electric field of the nucleus will <a href="/wiki/Pair_production" title="Pair production">pull an electron out of the vacuum</a>, resulting in the spontaneous emission of a <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The precise details of what happens to atoms with <i>Z</i> &gt; 173 are not known yet, but they probably should not survive long enough as such to be considered elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rsc_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-rsc-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_properties">Nuclear properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Nuclear properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Island_of_stability" title="Island of stability">island of stability</a> is expected to be centered on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unbibium" title="Unbibium">unbibium</a>-306 (with 122 protons and 184 neutrons),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kratz_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kratz-16">[16]</a></sup> and the second is expected to be centered on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unhexquadium" title="Unhexquadium">unhexquadium</a>-482 (with 164 protons and 318 neutrons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurekalert.org_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-eurekalert.org-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-18">[18]</a></sup> This second island of stability should confer additional stability on elements 152–168.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFricke_12-12"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Calculations according to the <a href="/wiki/Hartree%E2%80%93Fock_method" title="Hartree–Fock method">Hartree–Fock</a>–Bogoliubov Method using the non-relativistic Skyrme interaction have proposed Z=126 as a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_shell_model" title="Nuclear shell model">closed proton shell</a>. In this region of the periodic table, N=184 and N=196 have been suggested as closed neutron shells. Therefore, the isotopes of most interest are <sup>310</sup>Ubh and <sup>322</sup>Ubh, for these might be considerably longer-lived than other isotopes. Unbihexium, having a <a href="/wiki/Magic_number_(physics)" title="Magic number (physics)">magic number</a> of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a>, is predicted to be more stable than other elements in this region, and may have <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomers</a> with very long <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-lives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley2_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-emsley2-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electron_configurations">Electron configurations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Electron configurations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following are the expected electron configurations of elements 118–173. <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Attempts_to_synthesize_still_undiscovered_elements">Attempts to synthesize still undiscovered elements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Attempts to synthesize still undiscovered elements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Projects to build period 8 elements that have had synthesis attempts were elements 119, 120, 122, 124, 126, and 127. So far, none of these synthesis attempts were successful.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ununennium">Ununennium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Ununennium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The synthesis of ununennium was attempted in 1985 by bombarding a target of <a href="/wiki/Einsteinium" title="Einsteinium">einsteinium</a>-254 with <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>-48 ions at the superHILAC accelerator at Berkeley, California:</p>
<dl>

<p>No atoms were identified, leading to a limiting yield of 300 <a href="/wiki/Barn_(unit)" title="Barn (unit)">nb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> As of May 2012, plans are under way to attempt to synthesize the isotopes <sup>295</sup>Uue and <sup>296</sup>Uue by bombarding a target of <a href="/wiki/Berkelium" title="Berkelium">berkelium</a> with <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> at the <a href="/wiki/GSI_Helmholtz_Centre_for_Heavy_Ion_Research" title="GSI Helmholtz Centre for Heavy Ion Research">GSI Helmholtz Centre for Heavy Ion Research</a> in <a href="/wiki/Darmstadt" title="Darmstadt">Darmstadt</a>, Germany:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-economist-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duellmann_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duellmann-32">[32]</a></sup></p>

<p>The experiment was concluded in 2012, followed by the use of <sup>48</sup>Ca to irradiate the target, resulting in a confirmatory synthesis of element 117 (<a href="/wiki/Tennessine" title="Tennessine">tennessine</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> As of December 2015 the project's website described analysis of the element 119 data as ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unbinilium">Unbinilium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Unbinilium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Attempts to date to synthesize the element using fusion reactions at low excitation energy have met with failure; although, there are reports that the fission of nuclei of unbinilium at very high excitation has been successfully measured, indicating a strong shell effect at Z=120. In March–April 2007, the synthesis of unbinilium was attempted at the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Institute_for_Nuclear_Research" title="Joint Institute for Nuclear Research">Flerov Laboratory of Nuclear Reactions</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dubna" title="Dubna">Dubna</a> by bombarding a <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>-244 target with <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>-58 <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Initial analysis revealed that no atoms of element 120 were produced providing a limit of 400 <a href="/wiki/Barn_(unit)" title="Barn (unit)">fb</a> for the cross section at the energy studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>

<p>The Russian team are planning to upgrade their facilities before attempting the reaction again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duellmann_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Duellmann-32">[32]</a></sup></p>

The GSI repeated the experiment with higher sensitivity in three separate runs from April–May 2007, Jan–March 2008, and Sept–Oct 2008, all with negative results and providing a cross section limit of 90 fb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duellmann_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Duellmann-32">[32]</a></sup></p>

<p>In 2008, the team at GANIL, France, described the results from a new technique which attempts to measure the fission <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of a compound nucleus at high excitation energy, since the yields are significantly higher than from neutron evaporation channels. It is also a useful method for probing the effects of shell closures on the survivability of compound nuclei in the super-heavy region, which can indicate the exact position of the next proton shell (Z=114, 120, 124, or 126). The team studied the nuclear fusion reaction between uranium ions and a target of natural nickel:</p>

</dl>

</dl>
</dd>
</dl>

</dl>
<p>The results indicated that nuclei of unbinilium were produced at high (~70 MeV) excitation energy which underwent fission with measurable half-lives &gt; 10<sup>−18</sup> s. Although very short, the ability to measure such a process indicates a strong shell effect at Z=120. At lower excitation energy (see neutron evaporation), the effect of the shell will be enhanced and ground-state nuclei can be expected to have relatively long half-lives. This result could partially explain the relatively long half-life of <sup>294</sup>Og measured in experiments at Dubna. Similar experiments have indicated a similar phenomenon at Z=124 (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unbiquadium" title="Unbiquadium">unbiquadium</a>) but not for <a href="/wiki/Flerovium" title="Flerovium">flerovium</a>, suggesting that the next proton shell does in fact lie at Z&gt;120.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The team at RIKEN have begun a program utilizing <sup>248</sup>Cm targets and have indicated future experiments to probe the possibility of Z=120 being the next magic number using the aforementioned nuclear reactions to form <sup>302</sup>Ubn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unbibium">Unbibium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Unbibium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first attempt to synthesize unbibium was performed in 1972 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgy_Flerov" title="Georgy Flerov">Flerov</a> <i>et al.</i> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JINR" title="JINR">JINR</a>, using the hot fusion reaction:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msubsup>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>92</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>238</mn>
</mrow>
</msubsup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">U</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<msubsup>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>30</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<p>No atoms were detected and a yield limit of 5 <a href="/wiki/Barn_(unit)" title="Barn (unit)">mb</a> (5,000,000,000 <a href="/wiki/Barn_(unit)" title="Barn (unit)">pb</a>) was measured. Current results (see <a href="/wiki/Flerovium" title="Flerovium">flerovium</a>) have shown that the sensitivity of this experiment was too low by at least 6 orders of magnitude.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 2000, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gesellschaft_f%C3%BCr_Schwerionenforschung" title="Gesellschaft für Schwerionenforschung">Gesellschaft für Schwerionenforschung</a> (GSI) performed a very similar experiment with much higher sensitivity:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup></p>

<p>These results indicate that the synthesis of such heavier elements remains a significant challenge and further improvements of beam intensity and experimental efficiency is required. The sensitivity should be increased to 1 <a href="/wiki/Barn_(unit)" title="Barn (unit)">fb</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Another unsuccessful attempt to synthesize unbibium was carried out in 1978 at the GSI, where a natural <a href="/wiki/Erbium" title="Erbium">erbium</a> target was bombarded with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenon-136" title="Xenon-136">xenon-136</a> ions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup></p>

</dl>
<p>The two attempts in the 1970s to synthesize unbibium were caused by research investigating whether superheavy elements could potentially be naturally occurring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup> Several experiments have been performed between 2000-2004 at the Flerov laboratory of Nuclear Reactions studying the fission characteristics of the compound nucleus <sup>306</sup>Ubb. Two nuclear reactions have been used, namely <sup>248</sup>Cm + <sup>58</sup>Fe and <sup>242</sup>Pu + <sup>64</sup>Ni.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup> The results have revealed how nuclei such as this fission predominantly by expelling <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_shell_model" title="Nuclear shell model">closed shell</a> nuclei such as <sup>132</sup>Sn (Z=50, N=82). It was also found that the yield for the fusion-fission pathway was similar between <sup>48</sup>Ca and <sup>58</sup>Fe projectiles, indicating a possible future use of <sup>58</sup>Fe projectiles in superheavy element formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unbiquadium">Unbiquadium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Unbiquadium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a series of experiments, scientists at GANIL have attempted to measure the direct and delayed fission of compound nuclei of elements with Z=114, 120, and 124 in order to probe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_shell" title="Nuclear shell">shell</a> effects in this region and to pinpoint the next spherical proton shell. This is because having complete nuclear shells (or, equivalently, having a <a href="/wiki/Magic_number_(physics)" title="Magic number (physics)">magic number</a> of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>) would confer more stability on the nuclei of such superheavy elements, thus moving closer to the <a href="/wiki/Island_of_stability" title="Island of stability">island of stability</a>. In 2006, with full results published in 2008, the team provided results from a reaction involving the bombardment of a natural germanium target with uranium ions:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msubsup>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>92</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>238</mn>
</mrow>
</msubsup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">U</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<msubsup>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>a</mi>
<mi>t</mi>
</mrow>
</msubsup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
</msup>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">q</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>

<mi>n</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \,_{92}^{238}\mathrm {U} +\,_{32}^{nat}\mathrm {Ge} \to \,^{308,310,311,312,314}\mathrm {Ubq} ^{*}\to \ fission}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \,_{92}^{238}\mathrm {U} +\,_{32}^{nat}\mathrm {Ge} \to \,^{308,310,311,312,314}\mathrm {Ubq} ^{*}\to \ fission}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7587d197824002ac20a5f0514e093609c3160aa7" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:49.467ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The team reported that they had been able to identify compound nuclei fissioning with half-lives &gt; 10<sup>−18</sup> s. This result suggests a strong stabilizing effect at Z=124 and points to the next proton shell at Z&gt;120, not at Z=114 as previously thought. A compound nucleus is a loose combination of <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a> that have not arranged themselves into nuclear shells yet. It has no internal structure and is held together only by the collision forces between the target and projectile nuclei. It is estimated that it requires around 10<sup>−14</sup> s for the nucleons to arrange themselves into nuclear shells, at which point the compound nucleus becomes a <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclide</a>, and this number is used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUPAC" title="IUPAC">IUPAC</a> as the minimum <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> a claimed isotope must have to potentially be recognised as being discovered. Thus, the GANIL experiments do not count as a discovery of element 124.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-6"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unbihexium">Unbihexium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Unbihexium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first and only attempt to synthesize unbihexium, which was unsuccessful, was performed in 1971 at <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">CERN</a> by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Bimbot&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="René Bimbot (page does not exist)">René Bimbot</a> and John M. Alexander using the hot fusion reaction:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-7"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</dl>
<p>A high-energy <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a> was observed and taken as possible evidence for the synthesis of unbihexium. Recent research<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2011)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> suggests that this is highly unlikely as the sensitivity of experiments performed in 1971 would have been several orders of magnitude too low according to current understanding.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unbiseptium">Unbiseptium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Unbiseptium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_natural_occurrence">Possible natural occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Possible natural occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On April 24, 2008, a group led by <a href="/wiki/Amnon_Marinov" title="Amnon Marinov">Amnon Marinov</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_University_of_Jerusalem" title="Hebrew University of Jerusalem">Hebrew University of Jerusalem</a> claimed to have found single atoms of unbibium-292 in naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> deposits at an abundance of between 10<sup>−11</sup> and 10<sup>−12</sup>, relative to thorium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arxiv_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-arxiv-42">[42]</a></sup> The claim of Marinov <i>et al.</i> was criticized by a part of the scientific community, and Marinov says he has submitted the article to the journals <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_Physics" title="Nature Physics">Nature Physics</a></i> but both turned it down without sending it for peer review.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The unbibium-292 atoms were claimed to be <a href="/wiki/Superdeformation" title="Superdeformation">superdeformed</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hyperdeformation" title="Hyperdeformation">hyperdeformed</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">isomers</a>, with a half-life of at least 100 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-9"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>A criticism of the technique, previously used in purportedly identifying lighter <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> isotopes by mass spectrometry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thorium_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-thorium-44">[44]</a></sup> was published in Physical Review C in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> A rebuttal by the Marinov group was published in Physical Review C after the published comment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>A repeat of the thorium-experiment using the superior method of Accelerator Mass Spectrometry (AMS) failed to confirm the results, despite a 100-fold better sensitivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> This result throws considerable doubt on the results of the Marinov collaboration with regards to their claims of long-lived isotopes of <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thorium_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-thorium-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roentgenium" title="Roentgenium">roentgenium</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roentgenium_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-roentgenium-48">[48]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unbibium" title="Unbibium">unbibium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arxiv_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-arxiv-42">[42]</a></sup> It is still possible that traces of unbibium might only exist in some thorium samples, although this is unlikely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-10"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was suggested in 1976 that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primordial_element" title="Primordial element">primordial</a> superheavy elements (mainly <a href="/wiki/Livermorium" title="Livermorium">livermorium</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unbiquadium" title="Unbiquadium">unbiquadium</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unbihexium" title="Unbihexium">unbihexium</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unbiseptium" title="Unbiseptium">unbiseptium</a>) could be a cause of unexplained radiation damage in minerals. This prompted many researchers to search for them in nature from 1976 to 1983. Some claimed that they had detected alpha particles with the right energies to cause the damage observed, supporting the presence of these elements, while some claimed that none had been detected.</p>
<p>The possible extent of primordial superheavy elements on Earth today is uncertain. Even if they are confirmed to have caused the radiation damage long ago, they might now have decayed to mere traces, or even be completely gone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley2_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-emsley2-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extended_periodic_table&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
